1.00 INTRODUCTION
In accordance with our proposal dated June 19, 1992, GZA GeoEnvironmental, Inc.
(GZA) is pleased to present this report summarizing our geotechnical studies for the
reconstruction of the U.S. Route 1 Bridge N-10-15 over the Massachusetts Bay
Transportation Authority (MBTA) commuter rail right-of-way in Newbury,
Massachusetts. The purpose of the study was to evaluate the stability of proposed
embankments and retaining/proprietary wall systems, estimate probable settlements
of the proposed structure and roadway and to provide recommendations for
preliminary and final foundation design by others. The findings and recommendations
set forth in this report are subject to the Limitations outlined in Appendix A.
1.10 SUMMARY
Key findings and recommendations are:
1.
Soil conditions at the site generally consist of 5 to 10 feet of granular fill (not
including embankments), overlying 2 to 10 feet of clay/silt with traces of
organic silt and/or 1 to S feet of organic silt, overlying 0 to 45 feet of clay,
overlying 0 to 75 feet of interbedded clays/silts/sands, overlying 0 to 20 feet of
glacial till over bedrock. The clay and interbedded clays/silts/sands are
thickest at the proposed bridge locations and thinnest at the south end of the
approach embankment. Groundwater level is typically 2 to 10 feet below
existing ground surface (ie., from approximately elevation +6 National
Geodetic Vertical Datum (NGVD) to elevation +14 NGVD at the south and
north ends, respectively, of the road alignment).
It is recommended that the two new single-span bridges be supported on
footings bearing on compacted mechanically stabilized earth (MSE). The MSE
reinforcing should extend across the full width of the embankment in the areas
where new fill is being placed. Recommended maximum net allowable bearing
capacity for the abutment footing is 1.5 tons per square foot (tsf). The existing
fill below the MSE should be proofrolled prior to construction and unstable
areas or organics should be removed prior to constructing the wall. Abutment
footings should be at least 6 feet wide and the distance between the MSE wall
panels and front edge of footing should be at least 4 feet. This will allow for
a more equal distribution of lateral load on the wall.
Estimated total settlements of the two new bridges is 5 to 10 inches with
differential settlement of 2 to 4 inches from one abutment to another.
GZ\
Estimated settlement due to the raise-in-grade at existing embankments ranges
from 2 to 4 inches. Settlement of the middle proposed embankment is higher,
8 to 12 inches, since currently there is no fill in this area. Estimated
settlements are further discussed in Section 5.10.
These estimates assume that the embankments are constructed of granular fill
having a density of approximately 125 pounds per cubic foot (pcf) and that
where present, the existing timber piles help support the fill. If lightweight fill
(density of 60 pcef) were used in a zone behind the proposed wall, such that it
extended 20 feet horizontally from bottom of wall, and up to the roadway
surface at a 1-horizontal to 1-vertical slope, estimated total settlement of the
bridges is reduced by 2 to 3 inches.
About half of the settlement will occur within two years of placing the load;
most (about 90 percent) of the settlement will have occurred within ten years.
Wick drains could be installed to accelerate the rate of settlement and decrease
postconstruction settlement; however there would still be approximately 2 to
3 inches of total postconstruction settlement of the bridge abutments.
If estimated settlements are not tolerable, bridge abutments and retaining walls
could be supported on intermediate depth-driven piles or drilled shafts
supported below the clay in the interbedded clays/silts/sands. Drilled shafts
would have to be drilled using bentonite-grout or other slurry to help stabilize
the drill hole because of the layers/pockets of granular soils within the
interbedded deposits. The additional load carrying capacity gained by
supporting foundations in the glacial till and/or bedrock does not appear to be
warranted based on proposed bridge loads.
There would still be differential settlement (ranging from 5 to 12 inches)
between the bridge abutment and adjacent embankment fill, with the use of
intermediate depth or deep foundations. Thus, approach or hinged slabs would
be required to reduce the impact of differential settlement. Alternatively,
approach slabs could be designed and constructed to structurally span the
retaining walls leaving a void below the roadway’s structural deck or the
approach embankments could be constructed of lightweight fill.
Generally, the in situ soils at the site are not judged susceptible to liquefaction
based upon criteria set forth by Seed & Idriss (1983). However, there may be
localized pockets of loose granular soils within the clay/silt/sand stratum that
may possibly liquify during earthquake induced loading. However, these
pockets of potentially liquefiable soils are more than 50 feet below ground
surface and the amount of liquefaction would be limited by the high effective
Stresses. Since these potentially liquefiable soils are deep, ground surface
settlement associated with local liquefaction is expected to be negligible.
Z
GZ\
Recommended minimum lateral earth pressure for design of MSE retaining
walls is 40 pounds per square foot per foot (psf) of wall height. This assumes
a backfill unit weight of 125 pounds pcf and a friction angle of at least
30 degrees. Retaining walls must also be designed for appropriate seismic,
traffic, and bridge foundation surcharge loads. Estimated surcharge loads are
discussed in Section 5.40.
Gradation and electrochemical analyses on selected samples indicate the
existing embankment fill is variable in composition. Although the fill can be
reused to construct the new embankments (after stripping), its use will be
limited and will have to be monitored during construction. Electrochemical
tests will be required to approve fill used during construction behind MSE
walls.
1.20 SCOPE
To achieve the objectives of this study, the following work items were performed:
Reviewed previous engineering studies including a geotechnical report prepared
by the Massachusetts Department of Public Works (MDPW) entitled,
"Preliminary Geotechnical Report of Bridge No. N-10-15 in Newbury,
Massachusetts," dated February 1989 and other available subsurface data
including borings performed in the 1930s and 1988.
Planned, coordinated and conducted a subsurface exploration program
consisting of 20 test borings and eight test pits. A GZA engineer observed and
logged the explorations.
Performed laboratory testing on "undisturbed" tube samples obtained from the
clay stratum in two of the borings. The tests were performed to evaluate
strength and settlement characteristics of the clay deposit. In addition, we
performed index testing on select disturbed split spoon samples from clay and
silt deposits and interbedded clays/silts/sands deposits.
Performed laboratory gradation analyses and electrochemical analyses on
samples of the existing embankment fill from the borings and test pits.
Performed geotechnical engineering analyses including estimating settlements
and lateral earth pressures, evaluating seismic stability and slope stability, and
considering practical foundation alternatives.
Prepared this report summarizing results of the explorations and evaluations
and presenting our conclusions and recommendations.
2.00 BACKGROUND
2.10 SITE DESCRIPTION
The project site consists of a single multi-span reinforced concrete bridge and
approach embankments on Route 1 near the Newbury/Newburyport line. The bridge
is approximately 470 feet long by 65 feet wide; the south and north approach
embankments are each about 600 feet long. The bridge spans an abandoned railroad.
Presently, the existing bridge is closed to all pedestrian and motor vehicle traffic. An
at-grade detour roadway passes to the east around the structure. Several low wetland
areas parallel the bridge alignment and are within 25 feet of the existing approach
embankments. Site grades vary from about elevation +6 at the nearby wetlands to
+16 at the tracks to +42 at the top of the bridge. Elevations cited in this report are
referenced to the NGVD.
Refer to Figure 1 for a Site Locus and Figure 2 for a plan view of the structure.
hac
The existing structure provides for northbound and southbound roadways, each
approximately 30 feet wide. In addition, a 7-foot-wide pedestrian walkway is located
along the east side of the bridge, adjacent to the northbound travel lane. The bridge, ~
is supported at approximately 20 to 30 feet on center on reinforced concrete-bents,. on
which in turn are supported on below grade concrete pile caps and creosote-treated )
timber piles. The reinforced concrete piers and bridge deck exhibit-visible-signs of
deterioration such as spalling and corrosion. It is likely that the soil embankments
have settled since being constructed although there is no evidence of distress to the
embankments, which would indicate vertical or lateral movement.
The average length of the existing piles is estimated to be about 65 feet (based on the
total linear footage and total number of piles indicated on a copy of the design plans
prepared for the Boston and Maine Railroad entitled "Foundation Plan Bridge No. N-
10-15," dated 1935). A 65-foot-long pile would terminate in the silty sand or
interbedded clays/silts/sands below the clay layer. The plans also indicate a 20-ton
pile design capacity. However, pile lengths and capacities cannot-be confirmed since
pile installation logs are not available. Remnants of the piles were encountered during >,
test pit explorations, indicating the pile butts to be 16 to 18 inches in diameter. :
2.30 PROPOSED CONSTRUCTION
The two new replacement bridges will be constructed along approximately the same
alignment as the existing structure. The existing superstructure and the piers will be
4
GZ\
demolished but existing pile caps and piles will remain in place. The project bridge
engineer, Barnes & Jarnis, has developed several construction alternatives. At this
time, the preferred scheme involves constructing two single-span bridges, one over the
MBTA right-of-way and the second over an access road to the proposed commuter rail
parking lot located west of the roadway. Proposed abutment loads, based on
information received from Barnes & Jarnis on September 4, 1992 for the two bridges
are:
Load (kips per linear foot [kIf])
Bridge Live Load Dead Load
Railroad Span 2.4 15.4
Access Road Span 2.4 14.6
These loads include the weight of the new abutments.
The existing embankments would be extended and new fill placed where existing
concrete bents are now located. The roadway will be widened by approximately
18 feet on the west side and 10 feet on the east side. Retaining walls will be utilized
to limit encroachment on nearby wetlands and private property. MSE embankments
are proposed in lieu of conventional cast-in-place concrete retaining walls. North of
the existing railroad tracks, a ramp will be constructed paral o the northern
roadway approach embankment of the bridge to provide egress from the commuter
rail station. In addition, it is proposed to support the twe-bridges on footings bearing
on compacted MSE fill behind the walls, although this alternative would increase the
reinforcing requirements behind the wall.
Two alternative vertical profiles have been developed for the new roadway alignment:
a 45-mile-per-hour (mph) design speed and a 50-mph design speed. Both design
speeds involve raising the grade of the existing southerly embankment by 2 to 3 feet.
The northerly embankment would be raised about 4 feet and 10 feet for the 45- and
50-mph design speeds, respectively. With either profile a new embankment,
approximately 25-foot-high, will be constructed where the existing bridge is presently
located.
Additional earthwork and pavements will be designed by others for the proposed
commuter rail track, station complex, access road and parking facilities. This report
is not intended to address geotechnical issues for those components.

3.00 SUBSURFACE EXPLORATIONS
Subsurface explorations and laboratory analyses were conducted previously and during
the recent study, as discussed below.
3.10 PREVIOUS STUDIES
3.11 1930s Borings
Six test borings, designated boring Nos. 1 through 6, were performed in the
1930s for the design and construction of the existing bridge structure. Boring depth
varied between 62 and 125 feet, but generally extended 65 to 70 feet below the existing
grade of the railroad tracks. Boring information is available on the herein mentioned
1935 design plans.
3.12 1988 Borings
Six test borings (B-1 through B-4, and U-2 and U-3) were performed in 1988
by John J. Boyle and observed by Massachusetts Highway Department (MHD)
personnel. These borings were part of an initial study performed to provide
preliminary evaluation of the foundation alternatives for reconstruction of the existing
structure and embankments. Borings were extended between 43.5 and 153 feet below
the existing ground surface. Boring logs prepared by the driller are included in
Appendix B.
Eight undisturbed tube samples were obtained in the 1988 program for
laboratory testing. GZA performed index, consolidation and triaxial shear tests on the
undisturbed samples of clay, under contract to the MHD. Laboratory test procedures
and results are presented in Appendix C.
3.20 RECENT SUBSURFACE EXPLORATIONS
As part of this study, GZA conducted test borings, test pits and geotechnical
laboratory testing.
3.21 Test Borings
GZA Drilling, Inc. performed 20 test borings (designated as wall borings W-1
through W-8, including W-3A, and bridge borings BB-1, BB-2, BB-4, BB-6 through
BB-9 and BB-11 through BB-14) between July 20 and September 2, 1992. Wall
borings were located at approximately 200 feet on center along proposed embankment
retaining walls. Bridge borings were located at the ends, and in some cases, center of
the proposed bridge abutments. Boring locations are generally as shown on GZA’s
6
GZ\
letter to URS Consultants, Inc., dated June 24, 1992 except that three of the proposed
bridge borings were deleted from the exploration program, with the concurrence of
Barnes & Jarnis. Refer to Figure 2 for locations of borings as actually drilled.
Borings were drilled to depths of 18 to 137 feet below the existing ground
surface, and in all cases were terminated in either glacial till or bedrock. Eight
undisturbed tube samples of the clay were obtained in borings W-4-U and W-5-U for
laboratory testing. A minimum of 10 feet of rock core was obtained in five borings
(W-1, W-3, BB-4, BB-9, and BB-14). The purpose of the cores in the bridge borings
was to evaluate the quality of the bedrock in the event that deep foundations were
selected to support the proposed bridge and/or wall structures (cores were also
obtained in borings W-1 and W-3 since refusal was encountered at shallow depths).
Two observation wells were installed to monitor groundwater levels. The wells
consisted of 1.5-inch-diameter PVC wellscreen attached to solid PVC riser pipe. The
wellscreens were generally set to span the water table encountered in the boring
during drilling. Both wells were backfilled with Ottawa sand and complete with a
roadbox at the ground surface. A GZA engineer coordinated and observed the
exploration program and prepared boring logs included in Appendix D. Locations and
elevations of the borings were surveyed by GZA.
3.22 Test Pits
George Cairns and Sons, Inc. performed eight test pits (TP-1 through TP-7,
including TP-2A) on August 3, 1992. Test pits were excavated to depths ranging from
1.5 to 10.5 feet below the existing ground surface, and were terminated in the surficial
fill or naturally occurring clay stratum. Three of the test pits were performed adjacent
to the existing bridge piers to observe foundation conditions and four test pits were
performed in the existing embankments to collect samples for laboratory analyses. An
eighth test pit, TP-2A, was terminated at a depth of 1.5 feet after encountering a
buried wire. A GZA engineer observed the test pits and prepared the logs included
in Appendix E.
3.23 Laboratory Testing
The main purpose of the 1992 testing program was to assess the engineering
properties and characteristics of the clay stratum, and was developed to supplement
the 1988 laboratory program. It consisted of index tests (Atterberg limits, natural
water content, etc.) performed on both undisturbed tube and selected split spoon
samples, and six one-dimensional consolidation tests and three unconsolidated-
undrained triaxial shear tests on undisturbed tube samples. In addition, gradation and
electrochemical tests were conducted to help evaluate the existing embankment fill
for reuse as backfill behind MSE walls. Laboratory test procedures and results are
GZ\
presented in Appendix F; potential reuse of the existing fill is discussed in
Section 6.53.
4.00 SUBSURFACE CONDITIONS
4.10 SOIL PROFILE
In general, soil conditions at the site consist of 5 to 10 feet of granular fill overlying
2 to 10 feet of soft to stiff clay/silt with traces of organic silt or 1 to 5 feet of soft to
stiff organic silt, overlying 0 to 45 feet of stiff to soft clay, overlying 0 to 75 feet of
interbedded clays/silts/sands overlying 0 to 10 feet of glacial till over bedrock. Note
that depths and thicknesses discussed herein do not include height of the existing
embankment which ranges from about 5 to 30 feet. |
The soft to very stiff clay/silt with trace organics was encountered in five of the
borings below the existing embankments. The organic silt, which contained trace to
little amounts of fine sand was encountered in four of the bridge borings located away
from the existing embankments. Thus, it appears that some organic soils may have
been removed during original bridge construction and/or they could have been
consolidated under the weight of the existing embankments.
The clay and interbedded clays/silts/sands are thickest at the proposed bridge
locations and thinnest at the south end of the approach embankments. The
interbedded deposits contained 5- to 15-foot-thick pockets of silty sand, varying from
fine to medium sand with 30 to over 50 percent silt in some places.
The clay is typically 35 to 45 feet thick below the proposed bridges and northern
approach embankment. Based on consolidation tests the clay is overconsolidated
above approximately elevation -15 (upper approximately 20 feet of stratum). Below
a depth of about 30 feet (elevation -15), the clay is normally to slightly
overconsolidated. Laboratory torvane and triaxial strength tests indicate the upper
10 feet of the clay has an undrained shear strength of 1,000 to 3,000 psf. The
undrained strength decreases to about 400 psf with depth. The ratio of torvane tests
on undisturbed samples to tests on remolded samples range from 2 to 5 on clay
samples ranging in depth from 10 to 30 feet (above elevation -15), and 5 to 10 on clay
samples below a depth of 30 feet (below elevation -15), indicating the clay becomes
more sensitive with depth.
The top of bedrock is uneven and varies between about 5 and 132 feet below the
existing grade corresponding to elevations ranging from +10 at the southern end of
the alignment, to as deep as elevations -102 to -115 in the central area beneath the
GZ\
MBTA bridge railway alignment, and elevation -95 beneath the northern end of the
alignment.
Refer to Appendix G for a more detailed description of the soil strata and the logs in
Appendices B and D for details at individual boring locations. Figures 3 and 4 contain
subsurface profiles at proposed retaining wall and abutment locations.
Electrochemical tests of four samples of the fill are summarized in Table 1 and
discussed with respect to MSE walls in Section 6.53.
4.20 GROUNDWATER LEVELS
In the recent explorations, groundwater levels were measured during drilling or upon
completion of the boreholes, and after installation of the observation wells. In some
cases when there was not sufficient time for water levels to stabilize, the groundwater
levels were estimated based on the observed degree of saturation of the split spoon
samples.
Groundwater levels in August 1992 ranged from elevations +6 to +14, based on
observation well measurements (including measurements in two wells previously
installed at the site by others). These levels generally correspond to groundwater
levels observed in the 1992 borings and the 1988 borings. Observation well
measurements are summarized in Table 2.
Factors, including the permeability of the soil, cause a lag time between the creation
of the borehole or test pit and stabilization of the groundwater level. In general,
groundwater levels observed in wells that have stabilized for a period of time are
considered more reliable than levels observed in boreholes. Note that groundwater
levels encountered prior to, during, and after proposed construction may be different
due to variation in rainfall, temperature and other factors different from those existing
at the time the measurements were made.
5.00 GEOTECHNICAL ISSUES
The key geotechnical and foundation issues are:
. foundation type and capacity
. estimated settlement
° stability of embankment during construction
. type and stability of retaining walls
° impact of existing bridge and substructure (pile caps and piles) on behavior of
new bridges, walls and embankment fills.
GZ\
Each is discussed below. Also note that conceptual design sketches and calculations
are included in Appendices H and I, respectively.
5.10 FOUNDATION TYPE AND CAPACITY
The type of foundation selected to support each of the two new bridges will depend
on several factors: probable settlement patterns, stability of the embankment, loads
and relative costs. Four foundation types were evaluated:
. footings supported on MSE fill above the clay;
° deep piles and/or drilled shafts bearing in glacial till and/or bedrock;
. intermediate length piles or drilled shafts deriving capacity by friction in the
sands and silts above the till and bedrock; and
° shallow drilled shafts bearing near the top of the clay.
The advantages and disadvantages of each are discussed below.
5.11 Footings
Although the least expensive to construct, supporting each bridge on footings
will result in varying magnitudes of settlement. In addition to the settlement resulting
from fill being placed where there are no existing piles, footings will impose the bridge
loads onto the compressible clay layer, resulting in post-construction settlement of the
structure. The bearing elevation of the abutment footings relative to the top of the
clay layer impacts the magnitude of this additional settlement. Estimates indicate that
settlement resulting from footings bearing near the top of the approach embankments
(about 30 feet above the top of clay) would only be about 1 inch less than the
settlement resulting from footings bearing at a much lower grade near the top of the
existing clay, based on the proposed bridge dead load of approximately 15 kips/linear
foot. This is because the majority of settlement is the result of the embankment load
and most settlement occurs in the lower half of the clay layer, which is normally
consolidated. The increase in stress in the lower half of the clay is about the same
regardless of footing elevation. (Settlement is further discussed in Section 5.20.)
Footings bearing near to and behind the top of the retaining walls will also
impose lateral loads onto the wall, thus increasing the size and capacity requirements
of the MSE wall reinforcement. This lateral load can be more evenly distributed to
the wall by using wider footings (i.e., lower bearing pressure) and/or locating the toe
of footings further from the back of wall. In addition, if the footing is located close
to the wall, the bearing capacity of the MSE fill is reduced.
10
GZ\
5.12 Deep and Intermediate Length Foundations
Several types of deep and intermediate length foundations were considered to
support the proposed bridges below the clay layer; drilled shafts or end-bearing piles
driven to glacial till/bedrock, and piles or drilled shafts to the interbedded
clays/silts/sands. Each of these alternatives would be subject to downdrag loads
(negative skin friction) from the subsequent consolidation of the clay (and in some
places, organics), which will reduce their net load carrying capacity.
Prestressed, precast concrete piles are probably the most economical pile type
for deep, end bearing piles, based on our experience in the Boston area. Steel H or
pipe piles are typically $5 to $10 more per linear foot than prestressed concrete piles
for similar load carrying capacity. However, bidders should be given their choice of
pile type so that the most economical foundation is installed at the time of
construction. Axial capacity would mainly be a function of pile type, size, and length
and bearing stratum. For instance, piles bearing in glacial till and/or bedrock would
have higher capacities than piles bearing in the overlying interbedded clays/silts/sands.
Timber piles have limited structural design capacity typically in the range of 11
to 17 tons maximum, based on maximum allowable stress as provided in Table
4.5.7.3A of AASHTO for round timber piles. Capacity ranges are for typical pile
species (Eastern Spruce and Coastal Douglas Fir, respectively) and a pile tip diameter
of 6 inches. These capacities would be developed from friction in both the clay and
interbedded deposits; allowable capacity from friction and end bearing only in the
interbedded deposits is limited to 6 to 10 tons for 6- to 8-inch-diameter piles. For this
project, only friction and end bearing in the interbedded deposits can be utilized to
support the pile because of the proposed raise-in-grade to construct the roadway
embankments. Since the clay settles under the embankment load, it will cause
negative skin friction (downdrag) load, instead of helping to support the pile. This
negative skin friction is estimated to be equal to or greater than the pile capacity, thus
timber piles are not appropriate because of the raise-in-grade. Timber piles could be
used if the approach slabs were designed to structurally span the retaining walls
leaving a void below the roadway. For this scenario, there would not be negative skin
friction reducing the pile’s load carrying capacity.
Drilled shafts bearing in either the interbedded deposits or the underlying
glacial till/bedrock do not appear to be either practical or cost competitive. The
proposed loads for this project are too small for drilled shafts to the glacial till and/or
bedrock to be economical. Drilled shafts into bedrock are typically used for loads in
excess of 500 tons in the Boston area. Drilled shafts into either the interbedded
deposits or the glacial till would have to be drilled using a bentonite grout, or other
slurry, to help stabilize against granular soils collapsing into the hole. Drilling,
handling and disposal of the slurry increase the cost of drilled shafts. The cost per
foot for drilled shaft installation is more than double the cost per foot of pile driving.
11
GZ\
In addition, the existing piles and caps may obstruct the installation of a drilled
shaft more than installation of a pile. New piles can be located between existing piles
whereas existing piles may have to be removed to accommodate the larger diameter
drilled shaft.
Recommended pile types and capacities are presented in Section 6.12 and
Table 3.
5.13 Shallow Drilled Shafts Bearing Near the Top of the Clay
Shallow drilled shafts bearing near the top of the clay stratum would undergo
a similar magnitude of settlement as footings. Thus, the shallow drilled shafts do not
offer a performance advantage over footings. In addition, drilled shafts are anticipated
to cost more than footings. Installation would require some pre-excavation of existing
pile caps, and timber piles would have to be pulled and the resulting hole immediately
filled. This special procedure probably would be required to deal with the pile cap
and pile obstructions, and therefore, would probably increase costs above typical
drilled shaft installation costs.
5.20 SETTLEMENT
It is proposed that heights of fill varying from 3 feet to as much as 28 feet will be
placed to construct the new roadway embankments. This fill will be from the
following raises in grade:
. extending the approach embankments closer to the railroad track (and thus
placing fill beneath the shadow of the existing multi-span structure);
° raising the grade of the approaches, by up to 6 feet on the north approach and
4 feet on the south approach;
. constructing the northbound approach ramp on the east side of the roadway;
and
° widening the approach embankments.
These highly variable grade changes will result in a complex pattern of settlements of
the constructed embankments, retaining walls and bridge foundations. The magnitude
of settlement will depend on the amount of new fill placed, the presence of timber
piles below the area filled, the heights of the existing embankment, ‘the arching effect ~
— of the: MSE fill, fill, and the load of the new bridges (if supported on footings).
12
—S —
5.21 Settlement Estimates
Settlements were estimated for two conditions considered to be the extreme
limits to reflect the most likely postconstruction behavior:
° no existing piles to help support new soil load; and
° existing piles support wedge of soil above pile cap.
Although total estimated settlements are less if it is assumed that the existing
piles help support the fill, the estimated differential settlements are greater than if
there was no impact from the timber piles.

5.22 Use of Lightweight Fill
Lightweight aggregate fill and/or low density concrete fill could be used to
reduce the probable amounts of total settlement. Lightweight aggregate fill weighs
about 60 pounds pcf; low density concrete fill typically weighs about 30 to 35 pef. Use
of either of these materials would reduce the increase in load on the clay from the
new embankments. MSE reinforcing strips are not required where low density
concrete fill is used; the facade panels would be attached directly to the lightweight
concrete.
Settlements were estimated assuming lightweight fill was used below the bridge
abutments in a zone extending 20 feet out from bottom of the wall and up to the road
surface at a 1-horizontal to 1-vertical slope. Estimated settlement of the bridge
abutments would be reduced to about 5 to 7 inches (compared to 7 to 10 inches),
assuming no influence from existing timber piles. However, estimated settlement
within the center embankment, at a distance of 40 to 50 feet from the bridge abutment
is still 8 to 10 inches. Therefore, the use of a limited amount of lightweight fill could
increase differential settlement of the road surface. The limited area in which
lightweight fill would be used reduces its effectiveness in reducing settlement.
5.23 Rate of Settlement
The rate of settlement is also important. It is anticipated that about 90 percent
of the settlement will occur within approximately 10 years of when the embankments
are constructed and about half of the settlement will occur within two years. The rate
of settlement can be increased by using wick drains, decreasing postconstruction
settlement. Installation of the wick drains could be expensive and time consuming.
In addition, due to increasing environmental concerns of connecting upper and lower
aquifers, wick drains should not be installed through the entire clay deposit and should
be stopped at least 5 feet above the interbedded deposits.
Preliminary estimates indicate that wick drains installed on an approximately
S- by 5-foot grid would reduce the time for approximately 90 percent of the
consolidation to occur from approximately 10 years to approximately 1 to 2 months.
However, there still would be some postconstruction total settlement of about 2 to
3 inches, partially from bridge loads and partially from settlement of the lowest 5 feet
of the deposit where the wicks do not penetrate.
5.30 STABILITY OF EMBANKMENT DURING CONSTRUCTION
Up to 28-foot-high embankments will be constructed, as previously discussed. Because
of the low shear strength of the clay, there was a concern of a deep rotational failure
during construction. However, based on analyses, there is a factor of safety of at least
1.1 against a rotational failure for a 28-foot-high embankment excluding seismic loads.
14
GZ\
(Most raises-in-grade are less than 28 feet, estimated factor of safety for 25-foot-high
embankment is 1.3.) This appears reasonable considering that 18- to 22-foot-high
embankments have already been constructed for the existing bridge,/without reported
incidence. \ Since the factor of safety is near 1, there will be horizontal and vertical
~<“deformation of the fill as the embankment is constructed, which should be monitored
as discussed in Section 6.54.
The estimated factor of safety including the design earthquake load (A=0.17g) is less
than 1, assuming there has been no strength gain in the clay due to the embankment
load. Deformations of the embankment would be anticipated under design earthquake
conditions.
Conservative assumptions were used in the analyses; i.e., the fill is placed all at once,
undrained strengths of the clay, and there are no existing piles in the ground. A
higher factor of safety could be achieved if sequenced placing of fill was included
and/or wick drains were used to increase the rate of settlement. However, the overall
construction schedule for the commuter rail reconstruction may not permit a
sequenced loading procedure. The existing timber piles increase stability since they
act as vertical reinforcements through the clay, although it is difficult to evaluate the
extent of this benefit.
The potential for a shallow failure through the organic silt encountered in some of the
bridge borings was evaluated. However, since the organics do not appear to be
continuous, and will be removed (if present and/or unstable) from below the MSE
leveling pad, a potential failure plane through the soft organic silt should not be
present. Also, a stability problem through the organics is not anticipated in areas
below the existing embankment where organics were encountered. Except for the
organics in boring W-6, the organics in the remaining wall borings were mixed with
medium stiff to very stiff clays and generally comprised less than 10 percent of the
samples.
5.40 RETAINING WALLS
The type of retaining wall selected to support the embankments will depend on cost,
and time required for its construction. A flexible wall system is required because of
the anticipated magnitudes of total and differential settlements.
Cast-in-place concrete retaining walls (both gravity or cantilever-type) are more
sensitive to settlement than MSE or other proprietary walls. However, the sensitivity
to settlement of proprietary wall types varies. For instance, Tensitor® walls are highly
sensitive whereas Doublewal’ and MSE walls are only moderately sensitive. For
proprietary walls, the base width is about one-half to three-fourths of the height, the
minimum base to height ratio for MSE walls is 0.7 per ASSHTO. These ratios do not
include the impact of footing loads near the top of the walls; the base to height ratio
15
GZ\
will tend to be higher (about 1) for the proposed MSE abutments. Construction of all
these wall types will require some excavation of the existing embankment. A wall
system that minimizes excavation of the existing embankment and allows reuse of
existing soils is preferable.
Support of the bridges also may vary with wall type. For a gravity wall system, such
as a conventional cast-in-place wall or a Doublewal” system, the bridge loading can be
supported and transferred to the base of the wall, and thus, in effect become high
abutments. For an MSE wall, the bridge can be supported by independent footings
or piles behind the wall. The lateral load imposed on an MSE wall by footings
bearing above the base of the wall and traffic surcharge loads must be considered
during design, and will increase the length and decrease the spacing of reinforcing
strips required.
Seismic stability of the wall is also an issue since footings bearing above the base of
the wall (such as with an MSE wall) are used to support the bridge. Preliminary
evaluation shows that an MSE wall system will be stable under the design earthquake
(acceleration coefficient of 0.17g) for the proposed bridge load. Analyses assumed
vertical traffic and bridge surcharge loads of 640 psf and 18 kIf, respectively.
In some areas of the site (north of approximately Station 197+00 and south of
Station 193+00), the toes of the existing embankment slopes are wider than the
proposed raised embankment. Thus, construction of a retaining wall will require
substantial overexcavation of existing fill. In these areas, soil nailing may be an
appropriate technical approach to reduce the amount of excavation and refilling. Soil
nailing is a technique where the contractor starts at the top of the wall and installs the
wall as the excavation proceeds downward. A 4- to 5-foot vertical cut is made into the
embankment and shotcrete applied to the exposed soil face. Soil nails, consisting of
steel reinforcing bars surrounded by cement grout, are installed in a similar manner
as traditional tieback anchors. However, the soil nails are more horizontal, are not
tensioned and are more closely spaced than tiebacks. (Typically soil nails are 4- to
6-foot on center.) Once the first row of soil nails is installed, the contractor would
excavate the next lower 4- to 5-foot vertical lift of soil and continue the process. For
this application, the reinforcing bars would have to be epoxy coated for corrosion
protection since the nails would be permanent supports. Permanent panels, designed
to match the MSE wall panels, would be placed over the shotcrete face for aesthetics.
Although soil nailing appears technically feasible, it may be difficult to connect the
wall panels to the shotcrete (refer to concept sketch in Appendix H) and there may
be high costs associated with mobilizing another, separate, construction method.
5.50 IMPACT OF EXISTING STRUCTURE ON NEW BRIDGE
The existing bridge structure and its timber pile foundation affect both the design and
the construction of the new structure. As previously discussed, these piles reduce total
16
GZ
settlement since they will support an indeterminable portion of the fill placed to
construct the new embankments. The piles also increase the overall stability of the
underlying clay against a deep-seated, rotational failure during embankment and
retaining wall construction.
The timber piles could also have negative impacts. There will be differential
settlement between portions of the embankment over the piles and those portions
without piles. In addition, if the new structure (bridges and retaining walls) were
supported on deep or intermediate depth foundations (either driven piles or drilled
shafts), some of the existing timber piles would have to be removed prior to installing
new foundations. This would add to construction time and costs. Alternatively, new
foundation locations could be adjusted (where possible) to avoid existing piles.
6.00 CONCLUSIONS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
6.10 FOUNDATION TYPE
6.11 Footings
Proposed bridge abutments and retaining walls are recommended to be
supported on footings bearing on MSE fill placed over existing natural soils. The
abutment footings bearing on MSE should be designed for a maximum net allowable
bearing pressure of 1.5 tsf. It is recommended that bridge footings be at least 6 feet
-wide and that the toe of footing be set back at least 4 feet behind the back of the
MSE wall. This is to help distribute the bridge loads more evenly to both the wall and
the underlying MSE.
Abutment footings (and the fills supporting these structures) will undergo
differential settlement, as discussed in Section 5.20. If the bridge superstructure
cannot tolerate the magnitude of estimated settlement, then the structure must be
supported on driven piles. However, this would result in significant differential
settlements between the bridge and embankments that would have to be addressed.
All footings for both the bridge abutments and the MSE walls must bear at
least 4 feet below the adjacent ground surface to provide frost protection.
6.12 Piles
If piles are found to be required, it is recommended that both the two bridges
and the embankment retaining walls be supported on piles of intermediate lengths
driven into the interbedded clays/silts/sands. The higher capacity derived by driving
17
GZ\
6.20 SETTLEMENT
piles into the underlying dense glacial till or bedrock is not warranted for this project,
based on the proposed loads.
Pile capacity will depend on pile type size, length, and the bearing material into
which it is driven. Net design capacity should account for downdrag load. Since actual
downdrag loads will depend on pile size and spacing, potential downdrag will have to
be reviewed during final design once pile layout is complete. However, for estimating
purposes, preliminary design loads of piles with perimeters of approximately 4 to 6 feet
should be reduced by 25 tons to account for downdrag, unless acceptable mitigating
procedures are taken.
The calculated ultimate and allowable axial pile capacities for piles driven to
different lengths into the interbedded clays/silts/sands are outlined on Table 3. The
Project Specifications should include that test (or indicator) piles be driven at the
beginning of construction to confirm the design pile lengths as required for the
production piles. Wave equation analyses should be performed during final design to
evaluate driving stresses, if pile foundations are selected. Refer to Section 5.11 -
Division I of AASHTO Standard Specifications for allowable stresses during pile
driving.
In addition, it is recommended that pile load tests be performed to evaluate the
capacity of piles. The tests can either be traditional static tests conducted on at least
three test piles, or dynamic tests conducted on at least ten test piles and on restrikes
of approximately 20 percent of the production piles. The dynamic testing method may
be less expensive and could offer scheduling advantages since the contractor would not
have to wait until the static test was complete (up to seven days or more) to continue
pile installation. CAPWAP analyses (computer analysis performed based on field
dynamic testing results) would be required in conjunction with dynamic tests to
evaluate the percentage of load being supported in the interbedded deposits.
/
Estimated settlements are discussed in Section 5.20. The anticipated differential
Settlement of the structure from one bridge abutment to another is anticipated-to be
tolerable, although this should be confirmed by the project structural engineer.
However, the differential settlement of the roadway embankments may result in an
uneven pavement surface. The use of wick drains should be considered to increase
the rate of consolidation, and reduce total postconstruction settlement of the bridge
abutments and center embankment to 2 to 3 inches.
Settlement should be monitored as the fill is placed as during the construction period
to compare the actual rate and magnitude of settlement with design predictions. The
monitoring can also be used to evaluate whether embankment construction may be
accelerated or should be delayed.
18
GZ\
6.30 SEISMIC ISSUES
6.31 Liquefaction
The soils at the site generally are not considered susceptible to earthquake-
induced liquefaction based on criteria set forth in Section 1113.4.1.3 of the
Massachusetts State Building Code and analyses performed in accordance with
procedures outlined by Seed et al (1983 and 1987)'. Low densities (blow counts per
foot) in the interbedded sand/silt/clay deposits are usually associated with clay layers,
which are not subject to liquefaction. However, there may be localized pockets of
loose interbedded sand or silt within this layer which may be subject to liquefaction.
Maximum estimated settlement of the liquefied layer of soil is less than 2 inches.
Since the soils subject to potential liquefaction are more than 50 feet below ground
surface, ground surface settlement associated with localized liquefaction is expected
to be negligible.
6.32 Seismic Design
The retaining walls and bridge abutments must be designed to resist
earthquake-induced lateral loads. Conditions at the site are judged to meet criteria
for a Soil Profile III, in accordance with Chapter 3 of AASHTO 1991 Interim Standard
Specifications for Seismic Design of Highway Bridges. Therefore, we recommend a
site coefficient of 1.5 for seismic design.
6.40 RETAINING WALLS
6.41 Wall Type
It is recommended that the relative costs of MSE walls and proprietary gravity
wall systems be evaluated for this project. Traditional cast-in-place cantilever or
gravity walls do not appear to be economical and, unless supported on piles, probably
could not tolerate the anticipated differential settlement. As previously discussed,
MSE walls offer the advantage of distributing vertical footing and fill loads more
evenly to the underlying soil and existing piles. In areas where new embankments are
built without existing fill, the MSE reinforcing should extend for the full width (east
to west) of the embankment.
1Tokimatsu, K., Seed, H. B., " Evaluation of Settlements in Sands Due to Earthquake Shaking,"
Journal of Geotechnical Engineering, August 1987, Vol. 113, No. 8.
Seed, H.B., Idriss, I.M., Arango, I., "Evaluation of Liquefaction Potential Using Field Performance
Data," Journal of Geotechnical Engineering, March 1983, Vol. 109, No. 3.
19
GZ\
In areas where the existing embankment is wider than the proposed raised
embankment, a portion of the existing embankment would have to be excavated to
construct an MSE or other type of retaining wall, it is recommended that soil nailing
be given as an option in the project specifications for construction of the wall. This
occurs along the lower portion (i.e., the bottom 1/2 to 1/3) of the embankment
between approximately Stations 198+00 through 199+50 on the east side of the
roadway and Stations 197+50 through 200+00 on the west side of the roadway. Soil
nailing would reduce the amount of excavation required to construct the wall and may
reduce the subsequent times for wall/embankment construction. The specifications
would have to require the contractor to provide a design of the soil nail wall, stamped
by a registered Professional Engineer, including details of connecting precast panels
to the wall.
42 teral Earth Pressures
Recommended minimum equivalent fluid pressure to calculate active lateral
earth pressures for design of MSE or traditional cast-in-place concrete retaining walls
is 40 pcf. This assumes that the wall is backfilled with free-draining Gravel Borrow
with a moist unit weight of 125 pcf and a minimum friction angle of 30 degrees.
Where the calculated earth pressure behind cast-in-place or gravity-type
proprietary walls is less than 300 psf, it should be increased to 300 psf to account for
stresses induced during compaction. For MSE walls, this 300 psf minimum stress is
not required since compaction-induced stresses are distributed by the reinforcing strips.
For all proprietary walls, design pressures and backfill requirements should also meet
manufacturer’s minimum standards.
In addition to lateral earth pressures, retaining walls must be designed to resist
earthquake induced seismic loads, traffic surcharge loads, and lateral loads imposed
by the bridge foundations. Also, refer to Section 5 of AASHTO Standard
Specifications for minimum requirements for retaining wall design.
6.50 CONSTRUCTION ISSUES
6.51 Subgrade Preparation
Existing organic soils (if present) should be removed from below and within the
zone of influence of proposed footings and/or below proposed leveling strip of MSE
walls. Organics are expected to be present in localized pockets where there are
existing embankments. Thus, during construction, test pits should be excavated at
approximately 25 feet on center below retaining walls to evaluate whether organics are
present. The zone of influence is defined as extending 1 foot laterally from bottom
edge of footing and then down at a 1-horizontal to 1-vertical slope to the top of the
natural clay bearing stratum. Final excavation to the clay should be by hand or with
20
GZ\
a smooth-faced backhoe bucket to limit disturbance of the clay. Project Specifications
should require the Contractor to provide details of the excavation sequence, including
earth support requirements to limit encroachment on wetlands and/or special
procedures for excavating and backfilling in the wet.
Where organics are not present below the leveling strip for MSE walls, the
existing fill should be proofrolled with at least four passes of a 10,000-pound vibratory
roller. Loose or unstable zones should be removed and replaced with compacted
Gravel Borrow.
Similarly, in areas where new fill is to be placed to raise or extend the approach
embankments, then the existing surface should be stripped of existing pavement or
cleared to remove existing topsoil and the exposed surface recompacted with at least
ten passes of a 10,000-pound (static drum weight) vibratory drum roller. Any loose
zones identified during this proofrolling operation should be removed and replaced
with compacted Gravel Borrow.
The existing pile caps and piles should be left in place except in cases where
an existing cap interferes with a footing for the proposed retaining wall. In these
instances, the cap should be removed as necessary, and a new reinforced leveling strip
or grade beam placed to span over the existing piles below the MSE wall. This will
help to distribute the loads to the existing piles. The existing treated timber piles
should not be pulled since that would create a void and loosen underlying soils.
Recommended fill types and compaction requirements are outlined in
Section 6.52 and Table 4.
6.52 Fill Placement and Compaction
Gravel Borrow for bridge foundations placed below foundations and behind
retaining walls must be placed and compacted in accordance with Massachusetts DPW
Standard Specifications. A summary of these requirements are presented in Table 4.
Fill should be placed in loose layers not more than 12 inches thick and
compacted with a 10,000-pound (static drum weight) vibratory drum roller. In
confined areas where groundwater is more than 1 foot below working grade, place only
6-inch layers and compact with approved, manually-operated, powered vibratory plate
or drum compactor. Crushed stone, for any required depth of more than 12 inches,
should be placed in 6-inch (confined area), or 12-inch (unconfined area) layers and
compacted to an unyielding surface.
Extreme care must be used when compacting adjacent to walls. Where walls
are buried on both sides, backfill and compaction should proceed on both sides of the
wall so that the difference in fill on either side of the wall does not exceed 2 feet.
21
GZ\
Where backfill is only on one side of a cast-in-place concrete retaining wall, use hand-
operated rollers or plate compactors weighing not more than 250 pounds within 5 feet
of the wall.
6.53 Reuse of Excavated Material
The existing embankment fill can be reused to construct the new embankments
provided it can be compacted to the minimum requirements outlined in Section 6.52.
Gradation tests show that the existing embankment fill is variable, ranging in gradation
from fine sand with 10 to 30 percent gravel and 5 to 15 percent silt to fine sand with
30 to 40 percent silt. The siltier material will be more difficult to compact and tends
to have poor drainage capabilities. Thus, it should not be used to backfill a cast-in-
place wall nor should it be used to backfill the reinforcing strips used to support a
MSE wall.
Electrochemical tests show that the existing embankment fill generally meets
typical requirements set by MSE manufacturers. However, one of the four samples
tested had a high total chloride content and a second sample had a low resistivity.
Off-site fill may be required to backfill the reinforcing strips of MSE walls or, as an
option a testing program must be included during construction to insure that the fill
material meets the MSE manufacturer’s requirements.
6.54 Monitorin
As discussed in Section 6.20, it is recommended that both settlement and lateral
movements be monitored during construction of the embankments, retaining walls and
bridges. This will allow the engineer to evaluate whether the actual rate and
magnitude of settlement matches or is similar to estimates. The project specifications
should require the Contractor to establish and survey eight to ten settlement points
and/or platforms for each embankment (i.e., north, middle, and south). In addition,
to evaluate settlement with depth, it is recommended multi-level piezometers be
installed at two locations for each embankment and that Sondex devices be installed
at two locations for each embankment. The Sondex would be installed with
inclinometers to monitor for the potential for a deep-seated stability failure in the clay.
The inclinometers (with Sondex) should be located near the toe, and outside the
limits, of the proposed fill to monitor lateral movement in the clay.
22
GZ\

MHD during construction of the embankments, walls, and abutments. In particular,
the following issues should be addressed during construction:
. Review of submittals of MSE design details and shop drawings

APPENDIX A
LIMITATIONS
GEOTECHNICAL LIMITATIONS
Explorations
1.
Review
The analyses and recommendations submitted in this report are based in part upon the data obtained
from subsurface explorations. The nature and extent of variations between these explorations may not
become evident until construction. If variations then appear evident, it will be necessary to reevaluate
the recommendations of this report.
The generalized soil profile described in the text is intended to convey trends in subsurface conditions.
The boundaries between strata are approximate and idealized and have been developed by
interpretations of widely spaced explorations and samples; actual soil transitions are probably more
erratic. For specific information, refer to the boring logs.
Water level readings have been made in the drill holes at times and under conditions stated on the
boring logs. These data have been reviewed and interpretations have been made in the text of this
report. However, it must be noted that fluctuations in the level of the groundwater may occur due to
variations in rainfall, temperature, and other factors occurring since the time measurements were made.
In the event that any changes in the nature, design or location of the proposed bridge, retaining walls,
and embankments are planned, the conclusions and recommendations contained in this report shall not
be considered valid unless the changes are reviewed and conclusions of this report modified or verified
in writing by GZA GeoEnvironmental, Inc (GZA). It is recommended that this firm be provided the
opportunity for a general review of final design and specifications in order that earthwork and
foundation recommendations may be properly interpreted and implemented in the design and
specifications.
Construction
5.
It is recommended that this firm be retained to provide soil engineering services during construction of
the excavation and foundation phases of the work. This is to observe compliance with the design
concepts, specifications, and recommendations and to allow design changes in the event that subsurface
conditions differ from those anticipated prior to start of construction.
Use of Report
6.
This report has been prepared for the exclusive use of URS Consultants, Inc. and Barnes and Jarnis,
Inc. for specific application to the Route 1 Bridge Reconstruction located in Newbury, Massachusetts
in accordance with generally accepted soil and foundation engineering practices. No other warranty,
express or implied, is made.
This soil and foundation engineering report has been prepared for this project by GZA. This report
is for design purposes only and is not sufficient to prepare an accurate bid. Contractors wishing a copy
of the report may secure it with the understanding that its scope is limited to design considerations only.

APPENDIX C
1988 LABORATORY TEST PROCEDURES AND RESULTS
The test specimen was placed on a closed and sealed triaxial
cell base, encased with a membrane and sealed by O-rings.
The desired cell pressure was then introduced and maintained
for 60 minutes prior to testing. The test was run under
strain control conditions, loading the Samples at a rate of
0.5+ percent per minute. The applied load was measured
through a proving ring and recorded a predetermined strain
readings. When failure was reached, the sample was removed
from the loading apparatus and sketch made of the specimen
at failure, and its content determined.
Applied pressures were corrected for the effects of membrane
in accordance with procedures outlined by H. B. Seed and J.
M. Duncan, "Corrections for Strength Test Date," Journal of
Soil Mechanics and Foundation Division, September 1967.
eels Mechanics and Foundation Division
Consolidated undrained triaxial test (CIU)
The procedure outlined under U.S. Army Corps of Engineers
Manual EM 1110-2-1906, Appendix X (R test) used as a guide
during testing. Test samples were performed on full
diameter samples (2.87-inch diameter and 5.75-inch length)
or were trimmed in a vertical lathe to dimensions of
approximately 2.00-inch diameter and 4.00-inch length.
Water contents were obtained from trimmings adjacent to the
test sample, the specimen's weight was determined, and its
dimensions verified.
After trimming, the test specimen was placed on a previously
de-aired triaxial cell base and porous) stone. Filter
strips, if applicable, were placed in contact with the
porous stone at hexagonal points of the specimen; a membrane
was added and the sample was sealed top on bottom by '0!
rings.
Samples were back pressured under a small effective stress
to create complete saturation of the samples. The chamber
pressure was then increased such that the desired
consolidation effective stress was obtained. This effective
consolidation stress was allowed to act for 24 hours+.
During the consolidation phase, readings of volume change
versus time were recorded.
After consolidation, the response of the soil samples was
checked by increasing the cell pressure and monitoring the
pore pressure. Where required, additional back pressure was.
applied so as to achieve a pore pressure response equal to
or greater than 95 percent.
Load application rates were estimated using the formula
described in "The Triaxial Test," A. W. Bishop and D. J.
Henkel, Edward Arnold Publisher, 1957, pp. 124-127, 204-206.
The specimens were loaded under strain-controlled conditions
allowing no drainage during shear. The applied load, pore
pressure change, and change in height of the samples were
recorded at regular intervals during the test. When 20
percent strain was reached, the Samples were removed from
the loading apparatus. A sketch of the sample was made, the
entire weight was obtained, and its water content .
determined.
Applied pressures were corrected for the effects of membrane
and filter strips in accordance with procedures outlined by
Seed and Duncan
4.0 Test Procedures for Combined Sieve and Hydrometer Analysis
—— rr ee me neg sieve and Hydrometer Analysis
When both sieve and hydrometer analyses are required a combined
mechanical analysis is performed. This procedure is, in part,
similar to ASTM's 2217-66 (wet preparation of soil sample for
grain-size analysis and determination of soil constants-B).
A representative portion of the minus No. 4 material was mixed
with water so as to form a thin homogeneous slurry. The fines
suspended in this slurry were then decanted into an empty
hydrometer jar, and the mixing-decanting process repeated until
most of the fines had been removed. Coarser fractions remaining
after the decantation were then oven dried and sieved through a
nest of screens (Nos. 10, 20, 40, 50, 100, anda 200). Any
material passing the No. 200 screen was added to the hydrometer
jar containing the finer fraction.
Hydrometer analysis of these fines was -performed in the
conventional manner. 7

LABORATORY TEST PROCEDURES
BRIDGE N10-15 ROUTE 1
NEWBURY, MASS.
G10736.6
ls The following tests were performed with the noted ASTM test
designation:
TEST ASTM DESIGNATION
Consolidation Properties D2435-80 (see Item 2)
Grain Size D422-63 (See Item 4)
Moisture Content D2216-80
Triaxial Compressive Tests (See Item 3)
Liquid and Plastic Limits D4318-84
2.0 Test Procedures for Consolidation Properties (Addenda
— eee ee eh lication Properties (Addenda)
The procedure outlined under ASTM designation D2435-70 was used
as a guide during consolidation testing. Undisturbed samples
were trimmed by means of a soil lathe, carving each specimen to
test dimensions of 2.50 inches diameter and 0.80 inch height.
The sample was weighed, placed in a consolidometer and there
seated under a load of 1/8 tons per square foot. This load was
sequentially doubled (1/8-1/4 tsf, 1/4-1/2 tsf, 1/2-1 tsf, etc.).
All of the_test samples were rebounded after the maximum past
pressure (0 VM) was defined. Unloading was accomplished in two
steps to 1/2 tsf.
Using the doubling sequence established during the initial
loading, the sample was then reloaded to a maximum of 16 tons per
square foot. Each load and/or unload was allowed to act fora
controlled time increment. In this case, the increment used was
the end of 24+ hours. During application of these and all other
loads, the change in thickness of the sample was recorded at
regular time intervals.
A final rebound sequence followed (16-4, 4-1, 1-1/4), and the
Sample was removed, weighed, and oven dried for water content
determination.
3.0 Test Procedures for Triaxial Compressive Strength of Soil
a. Unconsolidated undrained triaxial tests (UU)
The procedure outlined under ASTM Designation D2850-70 was
used as a guide during testing. Test samples were trimmed
in a vertical lathe to dimensions of approximately 2.00-inch
diameter and 4.00-inch length. Water contents were obtained
from trimmings adjacent to the test sample, the specimen's
weight was determined and its dimensions verified.

GZA GEQENVIRONMENTAL, INC.
ENGINEERS AND SCIENTISTS APPENDIX E-9 8.3
LABORATORY TEST PROCEDURES
ROUTE 1 BRIDGE
NEWBURY, MASS.
12982
1. The following tests were performed with the noted ASTM test
designation:
TEST ASTM DESIGNATION
Consolidation Properties D2435-91 (see Item 2)
Moisture Content D2216-80
Triaxial Compressive Tests (see Item 3)
Liquid and Plastic Limits D4318-84
2.0 Test Procedures for Consolidation Properties (Addenda)
The procedure outlined under ASTM designation D2435-91 Method B was
used as a guide during consolidation testing. Undisturbed samples
were trimmed by means of a soil lathe, carving each specimen to
test dimensions of 2.50 inches diameter and 0.80 inch height. The
sample was weighed, placed in a consolidometer and there seated
under a load of 1/8 tons per square foot. This load was
sequentially doubled (1/8-1/4 tsf, 1/4-1/2 tsf, 1/2-1 tsf, etc.).
One of the test samples was rebounded after the maximum past
pressure ( VM) was defined. Unloading was accomplished in two
steps to 1/2 tsf.
Using the doubling sequence established during the initial loading,
the sample was then reloaded to a maximum of 16 tons per square
foot. Each load and/or unload was allowed to act for a controlled
time increment. In this case, the increment used was the end of
24+ hours. During application of these and all other loads, the
change in thickness of the sample was recorded at regular time
intervals.
A final rebound sequence followed (16-4, 4-1, 1-1/4), and the
sample was removed, weighed, and oven dried for water content
determination.
3.0 Test Procedures for Triaxial Compressive Strength of Soil
Unconsolidated undrained triaxial tests (UU)
The procedure outlined under ASTM Designation D2850-87 was
used as a guide during testing. Test samples were trimmed in
a vertical lathe to dimensions of approximately 2.00-inch
diameter and 4.00-inch length. Water contents were obtained
from trimmings adjacent to the test sample, the specimen's
weight was determined and its dimensions verified.
The test specimen was placed on a closed and sealed triaxial
cell base, encased with a membrane and sealed by O-rings. The
desired cell pressure was then introduced and maintained for
60 minutes prior to testing. The test was run under strain
control conditions, loading the samples at a rate of 0.5+_
percent per minute. The applied load was measured through a
proving ring and recorded a predetermined strain readings.
When failure was reached, the sample was removed from the
loading apparatus and sketch made of the specimen at failure,
and its content determined.
Applied pressures were corrected for the effects of membrane
in accordance with procedures outlined by H. B. Seed and J. M.
Duncan, "Corrections for Strength Test Date," Journal of Soil
Mechanics and Foundation Division, September 1967.

4.5.6.7.1
for the time period relating to the maximum axial load
transfer to the pile. If necessary, negative skin friction
loads that cause excessive settlement may be reduced by
application of bitumen or other viscous coating to the
pile surfaces before installation.
4.5.6.7.2 Expansive Soil
Piles driven in swelling soils may be subjected to
uplift forces in the zone of seasonal moisture change.
Piles shall extend a sufficient distance into moisture—
stable soils to provide adequate resistance to swelling
uplift forces. In addition, sufficient Clearance shall be
provided between the ground surface and the underside
of pile caps or grade beams to preclude the application of
uplift loads at the pile cap. Uplift loads may be reduced
by application of bitumen or other viscous coatings to
the pile surface in the swelling zone.
4.5.6.8 Dynamic/Seismic Design
Refer to Guide Specifications for Seismic Design of
Highway Bridges (1983) (see Supplement A of Interim
Specifications-Bridges-1991) and Lam and Martin
(1986a; 1986b) for guidance regarding the design of
driven piles subjected to dynamic and seismic loads.
4.5.7 Structural Capacity of Pile Section
4.5.7.1 Load Capacity Requirements
Piles shall be designed as structural members capable
of safely supporting all loads imposed on them by the
structure or surrounding soil.
4.5.7.2 Piles Extending Above Ground Surface
For portions of piles in air or water, or in soil not capa-
ble of providing adequate lateral support throughout the
pile length to prevent buckling, the structural design
Provisions for compression members of Sections 8,9, 10
and 13 shall apply except: timber piles shall be designed
in accordance with Article 13.3 using the allowable unit
stresses given in Article 13.2 for lumber and in Table
4.5.7.3A.
4.5.7.3 Allowable Stresses in Piles
The maximum allowable stress on a pile shall not
exceed the following limits in severe subsurface condi-
tions. Where pile damage or deterioration is possible, it
may be prudent to use a lower stress level than the maxi-
mum allowable stress.

4.5.7.4 Cross-Section Adjustment for Corrosion
For concrete-filled pipe piles where corrosion may be
expected, ie inch shall be deducted from the shell thick-
ness to allow for reduction in section due to corrosion.
Area of shell shall be included in determining percent-
age of reinforcement, p.
4.5.7.5 Scour
The probable depth of scour shall be determined by
subsurface exploration and hydraulic studies as
described in Article 4.3.5. If heavy scour is expected,
consideration shall be given to designing the portion of
the pile that would be exposed as a column. In all cases,
the pile length shall be determined such that the design
structural load may be safely supported entirely below
the probable scour depth. The pile shall be of adequate
Cross-section to withstand the driving necessary to pene-
trate through the anticipated scour depth to the design
embedment.
4.5.8 Protection Against Corrosion and Abrasion
Where conditions of exposure warrant, concrete
encasement or other corrosion protection shall be used
on steel piles and steel shells. Exposed steel piles or steel
shells shall not be used in salt or brackish water, and only
with caution in fresh water. Where the piling is exposed
to the abrasive action of the bed load of materials, the
section shall be increased in thickness or positive protec-
tion shall be provided.
4.5.9 Wave Equation Analysis
The constructability of the pile foundation design
should be evaluated using a wave equation computer pro-
gram. The wave equation should be used to confirm that
the design pile section can be installed to the desired
depth, ultimate capacity, and within the allowable driv-
ing stress levels specified in Article 4.5.1] using an
appropriately sized driving system.
4.5.10 Dynamic Monitoring
Dynamic monitoring may be specified for piles
installed in difficult subsurface conditions such as soils
with obstructions and boulders, or a steeply sloping bed-
rock surface to evaluate compliance with structural pile
capacity. Dynamic monitoring may also be considered
for geotechnical capacity verification where the size of
the project or other limitations deter static load testing.
4.5.11 Maximum Allowable Driving Stresses
Maximum allowable driving stresses in pile material
for top driven piles shall not exceed the following limits:
0.90F, (Compression)

Driving stresses may be estimated by performing
wave equation analyses or by dynamic monitoring of
force and acceleration at the pile head during pile
driving.
4.5.12 Tolerable Movement
Tolerable axial and lateral displacement criteria for
driven pile foundations shall be developed by the struc-
tural engineer consistent with the function and type of
structure, fixity of bearings, anticipated service life, and
consequences of unacceptable displacements on the
structural performance. Driven pile displacement analy-
ses shall be based on the results of in-situ and/or labora-
tory testing to characterize the load deformation behav-
ior of the foundation materials. Refer to Article
4.4.7.2.5 for additional guidance regarding tolerable
vertical and horizontal movement criteria.

SEISMIC DESIGN OF RETAINING WALLS
David G. Elns!, M.ASCE and Rowland Richards, Jr., M.ASCE
ABSTRACT: A series of theoretical and experimental investiga-
tions is summarized on the seismic behavior of gravity, tied-back
and reinforced-earth retaining walls. The Newmark sliding-block
model is shown to be appropriate for displacement -controlled
design, though modifications and limitations are discussed. Test
results show that sliding block behavior only takes place after a
high limiting acceleration has been passed. Where rotational
failure is expected, walls will be stronger than anticipated.
Elastic resonance effects are likely to be significant for full-
size walls.
INTRODUCTION - - DISPLACEMENT-CONTROLLED DESIGN
In earlier papers (3, 19) a displacement-controlled approach
was developed for the seismic design of gravity retaining walls.
The work stemmed from a project (18) whose aim was to rationalize
Procedures for the earthquake design of retaining structures.
For gravity walls the problem was that the very mass that gave
stability in a static situation acted against the wall in an
earthquake. A pseudo-static force-based design approach required
that the wall should resist lateral forces due to (a) lateral
earth pressures, obtained by the Mononobe-Okabe method (12, 17),
and (b) the intertia effects of the wall. The lateral forces
were resisted primarily by the wall base resistance against
sliding or overturning, which of course depended on the wall
mass. The force approach led to unacceptably high values of mass
for stability.
However, it was shown (3, 19) that although in an earthquake a
wall with a mass lower than some critical value would displace,
the displacement was neither infinite nor indefinite. It was
finite and, for a given earthquake record, calculable.
The authors made the simple assumption that outward movement
of the wall would take place only when the lateral earthquake
acceleration away from the wall into the soil was greater than a
specific acceleration at which slipping of the wall would take
Place. It was also assumed that once sliding took place it would

RETAINING WALLS SEISMIC DESIGN
The process was simple, despite the rather complex equations.
Nevertheless, various questions needed to be answered. Firstly,
did failing walls actually behave in such a_ simple manner,
sliding with a constant cut-off acceleration coefficient?
Probably not exactly, because the analysis was based on the
simplifying Mononobe-Okabe assumptions of a uniform ¢, a plane
failure surface, uniform acceleration and no resonance. The
sliding block model assumed the soil and wall were subjected to
undirectional shaking and that the soil wedge moved horizon-
tally, which is geometrically impossible. In any case, the
design approach seemed only applicable to gravity walls failing
by sliding and could not be applied directly to other types of
walls or to alternative failure modes such as rotation about the
bottom. It was necessary to explore these matters before the
approach could be used with broad confidence. The following
sections describe some of the explorations.
GRAVITY WALLS FAILING BY SLIDING
Model Tests. Small-scale tests were carried out (1) with the
aim of confirming whether the simple sliding-block model was
justified. The test set-up is shown in Fig. 3. An 810 mm wall
whose mass could be varied was free to fail by sliding. It was
backfilled with beach sand, placed by raining in a reasonably
dense state. Excitation was by means of a large spring whose
release gave a decaying sinusoidal motion. Although a shake
table was available it was not used (except as a passive support)
as earlier tests (10) had shown it was impossible to get a noise-
free sinusoidal acceleration profile, and examination of
acceleration responses to simple excitations was critical to the
investigation.
